   <h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Elizabeth I of England</h1> 
   <div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content"> 
    <div id="siteSub" class="noprint">

    </div>

      <p><b>Elizabeth I</b> (7 September 1533 – 24 March 1603)<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> was <a href="/wiki/List_of_English_monarchs" title="List of English monarchs">Queen of England</a> and <a href="/wiki/Monarchy_of_Ireland" title="Monarchy of Ireland">Ireland</a> from 17 November 1558 until her death on 24 March 1603. Sometimes called <b>the Virgin Queen</b>, <b>Gloriana</b> or <b>Good Queen Bess</b>, Elizabeth was the last of the five monarchs of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Tudor" title="House of Tudor">House of Tudor</a>. </p>
      <p>Elizabeth was the daughter of <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII" class="mw-redirect" title="Henry VIII">Henry VIII</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anne_Boleyn" title="Anne Boleyn">Anne Boleyn</a>, his second wife, who was executed two-and-a-half years after Elizabeth's birth. Anne's marriage to Henry VIII was annulled, and Elizabeth was declared illegitimate. Her half-brother, <a href="/wiki/Edward_VI" class="mw-redirect" title="Edward VI">Edward VI</a>, ruled until his death in 1553, bequeathing the crown to <a href="/wiki/Lady_Jane_Grey" title="Lady Jane Grey">Lady Jane Grey</a> and ignoring the claims of his two half-sisters, Elizabeth and the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic">Roman Catholic</a> <a href="/wiki/Mary_I_of_England" title="Mary I of England">Mary</a>, in spite of <a href="/wiki/Third_Succession_Act" title="Third Succession Act">statute law to the contrary</a>. Edward's will was set aside and Mary became queen, deposing Lady Jane Grey. During Mary's reign, Elizabeth was imprisoned for nearly a year on suspicion of supporting Protestant rebels. </p>
      <p>In 1558 upon Mary's death, Elizabeth succeeded her half-sister to the throne and set out to rule by good counsel.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> She depended heavily on a group of trusted advisers, led by <a href="/wiki/William_Cecil,_1st_Baron_Burghley" title="William Cecil, 1st Baron Burghley">William Cecil, 1st Baron Burghley</a>. One of her first actions as queen was the establishment of an English Protestant church, of which she became the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Governor" class="mw-redirect" title="Supreme Governor">Supreme Governor</a>. This <a href="/wiki/Elizabethan_Religious_Settlement" title="Elizabethan Religious Settlement">Elizabethan Religious Settlement</a> was to evolve into the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>. It was expected that Elizabeth would marry and produce an heir; however, despite numerous courtships, she never did. She was eventually succeeded by her first cousin twice removed, <a href="/wiki/James_VI_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="James VI of Scotland">James VI of Scotland</a>. She had earlier been responsible for the imprisonment and execution of James's mother, <a href="/wiki/Mary,_Queen_of_Scots" title="Mary, Queen of Scots">Mary, Queen of Scots</a>. </p>
      <p>In government, Elizabeth was more moderate than her father and half-siblings had been.<sup id="cite_ref-starkey5_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-starkey5-3">[3]</a></sup> One of her mottoes was "<i>video et taceo</i>" ("I see but say nothing").<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> In religion, she was relatively tolerant and avoided systematic persecution. After the pope <a href="/wiki/Regnans_in_Excelsis" title="Regnans in Excelsis">declared her illegitimate</a> in 1570 and released her subjects from obedience to her, several conspiracies threatened her life, all of which were defeated with the help of her ministers' secret service. Elizabeth was cautious in foreign affairs, manoeuvring between the major powers of France and Spain. She only half-heartedly supported a number of ineffective, poorly resourced military campaigns in the Netherlands, France, and Ireland. By the mid-1580s, England could no longer avoid <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Spanish_War_(1585%E2%80%931604)" title="Anglo-Spanish War (1585–1604)">war with Spain</a>. England's defeat of the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Armada" title="Spanish Armada">Spanish Armada</a> in 1588 associated Elizabeth with one of the greatest military victories in English history. </p>
      <p>As she grew older, Elizabeth became celebrated for her <a href="/wiki/Virginity" title="Virginity">virginity</a>. A cult grew around her which was celebrated in the portraits, pageants, and literature of the day. Elizabeth's reign became known as the <a href="/wiki/Elizabethan_era" title="Elizabethan era">Elizabethan era</a>. The period is famous for the flourishing of <a href="/wiki/English_Renaissance_theatre" title="English Renaissance theatre">English drama</a>, led by playwrights such as <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Marlowe" title="Christopher Marlowe">Christopher Marlowe</a>, and for the seafaring prowess of English adventurers such as <a href="/wiki/Francis_Drake" title="Francis Drake">Francis Drake</a>. Some historians depict Elizabeth as a short-tempered, sometimes indecisive ruler,<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> who enjoyed more than her share of luck. Towards the end of her reign, a series of economic and military problems weakened her popularity. Elizabeth is acknowledged as a charismatic performer and a dogged survivor in an era when government was ramshackle and limited, and when monarchs in neighbouring countries faced internal problems that jeopardised their thrones. After the short reigns of her half-siblings, her 44 years on the throne provided welcome stability for the kingdom and helped forge a sense of national identity.<sup id="cite_ref-starkey5_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-starkey5-3">[3]</a></sup> </p>

         </ul> </li> 
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#Later_years"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Later years</span></a></li> 
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#Death"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Death</span></a></li> 
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20"><a href="#Legacy"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Legacy</span></a></li>

      </div> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span></h2> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Henry_VIII_and_Anne_Boleyn.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Henry_VIII_and_Anne_Boleyn.png/220px-Henry_VIII_and_Anne_Boleyn.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Henry_VIII_and_Anne_Boleyn.png/330px-Henry_VIII_and_Anne_Boleyn.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Henry_VIII_and_Anne_Boleyn.png/440px-Henry_VIII_and_Anne_Boleyn.png 2x" data-file-width="846" data-file-height="564"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Henry_VIII_and_Anne_Boleyn.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Elizabeth's parents, Henry VIII and Anne Boleyn. Anne was executed less than three years after Elizabeth's birth.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Elizabeth was born at <a href="/wiki/Greenwich_Palace" class="mw-redirect" title="Greenwich Palace">Greenwich Palace</a> and was named after her grandmothers, <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_of_York" title="Elizabeth of York">Elizabeth of York</a> and <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Boleyn,_Countess_of_Wiltshire" title="Elizabeth Boleyn, Countess of Wiltshire">Elizabeth Howard</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> She was the second child of <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">Henry VIII of England</a> born in wedlock to survive infancy. Her mother was Henry's second wife, <a href="/wiki/Anne_Boleyn" title="Anne Boleyn">Anne Boleyn</a>. At birth, Elizabeth was the <a href="/wiki/Heir_presumptive" title="Heir presumptive">heir presumptive</a> to the throne of England. Her older half-sister, <a href="/wiki/Mary_I_of_England" title="Mary I of England">Mary</a>, had lost her position as a legitimate heir when Henry annulled his marriage to Mary's mother, <a href="/wiki/Catherine_of_Aragon" title="Catherine of Aragon">Catherine of Aragon</a>, to marry Anne, with the intent to sire a male heir and ensure the Tudor succession.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> She was baptised on 10 September 1533; Archbishop <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Cranmer" title="Thomas Cranmer">Thomas Cranmer</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Henry_Courtenay,_1st_Marquess_of_Exeter" title="Henry Courtenay, 1st Marquess of Exeter">Marquess of Exeter</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Stafford,_Duchess_of_Norfolk" title="Elizabeth Stafford, Duchess of Norfolk">Duchess of Norfolk</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Wotton,_Marchioness_of_Dorset" title="Margaret Wotton, Marchioness of Dorset">Dowager Marchioness of Dorset</a> stood as her godparents. A canopy was carried at the ceremony over the three-day old child by her uncle <a href="/wiki/George_Boleyn,_Viscount_Rochford" class="mw-redirect" title="George Boleyn, Viscount Rochford">Viscount Rochford</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Hussey,_1st_Baron_Hussey_of_Sleaford" title="John Hussey, 1st Baron Hussey of Sleaford">Lord Hussey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lord_Thomas_Howard" title="Lord Thomas Howard">Lord Thomas Howard</a>, and <a href="/wiki/William_Howard,_1st_Baron_Howard_of_Effingham" title="William Howard, 1st Baron Howard of Effingham">Lord Howard of Effingham</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Elizabeth was two years and eight months old when her mother was beheaded on 19 May 1536,<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> four months after Catherine of Aragon's death from natural causes. Elizabeth was declared illegitimate and deprived of her place in the royal succession.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Eleven days after Anne Boleyn's execution, Henry married <a href="/wiki/Jane_Seymour" title="Jane Seymour">Jane Seymour</a>, who died shortly after the birth of their son, <a href="/wiki/Edward_VI_of_England" title="Edward VI of England">Edward</a>, in 1537. From his birth, Edward was undisputed <a href="/wiki/Heir_apparent" title="Heir apparent">heir apparent</a> to the throne. Elizabeth was placed in his household and carried the <a href="/wiki/Chrisom" title="Chrisom">chrisom</a>, or baptismal cloth, at his christening.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth_I_when_a_Princess.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Elizabeth_I_when_a_Princess.jpg/220px-Elizabeth_I_when_a_Princess.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="290" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Elizabeth_I_when_a_Princess.jpg/330px-Elizabeth_I_when_a_Princess.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Elizabeth_I_when_a_Princess.jpg/440px-Elizabeth_I_when_a_Princess.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1517" data-file-height="2000"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth_I_when_a_Princess.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A rare portrait of Elizabeth prior to her accession, attributed to 
         <a href="/wiki/William_Scrots" title="William Scrots">William Scrots</a>. It was painted for her father in 
         <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 1546.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Elizabeth's first <a href="/wiki/Governess" title="Governess">governess</a>, <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Bryan" title="Margaret Bryan">Margaret Bryan</a>, wrote that she was "as toward a child and as gentle of conditions as ever I knew any in my life".<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Catherine_Champernowne" class="mw-redirect" title="Catherine Champernowne">Catherine Champernowne</a>, better known by her later, married name of Catherine "Kat" Ashley, was appointed as Elizabeth's governess in 1537, and she remained Elizabeth's friend until her death in 1565. Champernowne taught Elizabeth four languages: French, <a href="/wiki/Flemish_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Flemish language">Flemish</a>, Italian and Spanish.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> By the time William Grindal became her tutor in 1544, Elizabeth could write English, <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>, and Italian. Under Grindal, a talented and skilful tutor, she also progressed in French and Greek.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> After Grindal died in 1548, Elizabeth received her education under <a href="/wiki/Roger_Ascham" title="Roger Ascham">Roger Ascham</a>, a sympathetic teacher who believed that learning should be engaging.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>By the time her formal education ended in 1550, Elizabeth was one of the best educated women of her generation.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> At the end of her life, Elizabeth was also believed to speak <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cornish_language" title="Cornish language">Cornish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scots_language" title="Scots language">Scottish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Irish_language" title="Irish language">Irish</a> in addition to the languages mentioned above. The Venetian ambassador stated in 1603 that she "possessed [these] languages so thoroughly that each appeared to be her native tongue".<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Historian Mark Stoyle suggests that she was probably taught Cornish by <a href="/wiki/William_Killigrew_(Chamberlain_of_the_Exchequer)" title="William Killigrew (Chamberlain of the Exchequer)">William Killigrew</a>, Groom of the Privy Chamber and later Chamberlain of the Exchequer.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Thomas_Seymour">Thomas Seymour</span></h2> 
      <p>Henry VIII died in 1547 and Elizabeth's half-brother, Edward VI, became king at age nine. <a href="/wiki/Catherine_Parr" title="Catherine Parr">Catherine Parr</a>, Henry's widow, soon married <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Seymour,_1st_Baron_Seymour_of_Sudeley" title="Thomas Seymour, 1st Baron Seymour of Sudeley">Thomas Seymour, 1st Baron Seymour of Sudeley</a>, Edward VI's uncle and the brother of the Lord Protector, <a href="/wiki/Edward_Seymour,_1st_Duke_of_Somerset" title="Edward Seymour, 1st Duke of Somerset">Edward Seymour, 1st Duke of Somerset</a>. The couple took Elizabeth into their household at <a href="/wiki/Chelsea,_London" title="Chelsea, London">Chelsea</a>. There Elizabeth experienced an emotional crisis that some historians believe affected her for the rest of her life.<sup id="cite_ref-loades2_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-loades2-20">[20]</a></sup> Thomas Seymour, approaching age 40 but having charm and "a powerful sex appeal",<sup id="cite_ref-loades2_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-loades2-20">[20]</a></sup> engaged in romps and horseplay with the 14-year-old Elizabeth. These included entering her bedroom in his nightgown, tickling her and slapping her on the buttocks. Parr, rather than confront her husband over his inappropriate activities, joined in. Twice she accompanied him in tickling Elizabeth, and once held her while he cut her black gown "into a thousand pieces."<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> However, after Parr discovered the pair in an embrace, she ended this state of affairs.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> In May 1548, Elizabeth was sent away. </p>
      <p>However, Thomas Seymour continued scheming to control the royal family and tried to have himself appointed the governor of the King's person.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> When Parr died after childbirth on 5 September 1548, he renewed his attentions towards Elizabeth, intent on marrying her.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> The details of his former behaviour towards Elizabeth emerged,<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> and for his brother and the <a href="/wiki/Privy_Council_of_England" title="Privy Council of England">king's council</a>, this was the last straw.<sup id="cite_ref-neale_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-neale-27">[27]</a></sup> In January 1549, Seymour was arrested on suspicion of plotting to marry Elizabeth and overthrow the Lord Protector. Elizabeth, living at <a href="/wiki/Hatfield_House" title="Hatfield House">Hatfield House</a>, would admit nothing. Her stubbornness exasperated her interrogator, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Tyrwhitt_(courtier)" title="Robert Tyrwhitt (courtier)">Sir Robert Tyrwhitt</a>, who reported, "I do see it in her face that she is guilty".<sup id="cite_ref-neale_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-neale-27">[27]</a></sup> Seymour was beheaded on 20 March 1549. </p> 
      <h2><span id="Mary_I.27s_reign"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Mary_I's_reign">Mary I's reign</span></h2> 
      <p>Edward VI died on 6 July 1553, aged 15. His will ignored the <a href="/wiki/Succession_to_the_Crown_Act_1543" class="mw-redirect" title="Succession to the Crown Act 1543">Succession to the Crown Act 1543</a>, excluded both Mary and Elizabeth from the succession, and instead declared as his heir <a href="/wiki/Lady_Jane_Grey" title="Lady Jane Grey">Lady Jane Grey</a>, granddaughter of Henry VIII's younger sister, <a href="/wiki/Mary_Tudor,_Queen_of_France" title="Mary Tudor, Queen of France">Mary</a>. Jane was proclaimed queen by the Privy Council, but her support quickly crumbled, and she was deposed after nine days. On 3 August 1553, Mary rode triumphantly into London, with Elizabeth at her side.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Felipe_of_Spain_and_MariaTudor-2010-13-09.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Felipe_of_Spain_and_MariaTudor-2010-13-09.jpg/220px-Felipe_of_Spain_and_MariaTudor-2010-13-09.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="202" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Felipe_of_Spain_and_MariaTudor-2010-13-09.jpg/330px-Felipe_of_Spain_and_MariaTudor-2010-13-09.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7f/Felipe_of_Spain_and_MariaTudor-2010-13-09.jpg 2x" data-file-width="424" data-file-height="390"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Felipe_of_Spain_and_MariaTudor-2010-13-09.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Philip and Mary I, during whose reign Elizabeth was heir presumptive
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The show of solidarity between the sisters did not last long. Mary, a devout Catholic, was determined to crush the Protestant faith in which Elizabeth had been educated, and she ordered that everyone attend Catholic Mass; Elizabeth had to outwardly conform. Mary's initial popularity ebbed away in 1554 when she announced plans to marry <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Spain" title="Philip II of Spain">Philip of Spain</a>, the son of <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Emperor_Charles_V" class="mw-redirect" title="Holy Roman Emperor Charles V">Holy Roman Emperor Charles V</a> and an active Catholic.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Discontent spread rapidly through the country, and many looked to Elizabeth as a focus for their opposition to Mary's religious policies. </p>
      <p>In January and February 1554, <a href="/wiki/Wyatt%27s_rebellion" title="Wyatt's rebellion">Wyatt's rebellion</a> broke out; it was soon suppressed.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Elizabeth was brought to court, and interrogated regarding her role, and on 18 March, she was imprisoned in the <a href="/wiki/Tower_of_London" title="Tower of London">Tower of London</a>. Elizabeth fervently protested her innocence.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Though it is unlikely that she had plotted with the rebels, some of them were known to have approached her. Mary's closest confidant, Charles V's ambassador <a href="/wiki/Simon_Renard" title="Simon Renard">Simon Renard</a>, argued that her throne would never be safe while Elizabeth lived; and the Chancellor, <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Gardiner" title="Stephen Gardiner">Stephen Gardiner</a>, worked to have Elizabeth put on trial.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Elizabeth's supporters in the government, including <a href="/wiki/William_Paget,_1st_Baron_Paget" title="William Paget, 1st Baron Paget">Lord Paget</a>, convinced Mary to spare her sister in the absence of hard evidence against her. Instead, on 22 May, Elizabeth was moved from the Tower to <a href="/wiki/Woodstock,_Oxfordshire" title="Woodstock, Oxfordshire">Woodstock</a>, where she was to spend almost a year under house arrest in the charge of Sir <a href="/wiki/Henry_Bedingfeld" title="Henry Bedingfeld">Henry Bedingfield</a>. Crowds cheered her all along the way.<sup id="cite_ref-loades29_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-loades29-33">[33]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Hatfield_House_Old_Palace.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Hatfield_House_Old_Palace.jpg/220px-Hatfield_House_Old_Palace.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Hatfield_House_Old_Palace.jpg/330px-Hatfield_House_Old_Palace.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Hatfield_House_Old_Palace.jpg/440px-Hatfield_House_Old_Palace.jpg 2x" data-file-width="693" data-file-height="461"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Hatfield_House_Old_Palace.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Hatfield House, where Elizabeth lived during Mary's reign
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>On 17 April 1555, Elizabeth was recalled to court to attend the final stages of Mary's <a href="/wiki/Mary_I_of_England#False_pregnancy" title="Mary I of England">apparent pregnancy</a>. If Mary and her child died, Elizabeth would become queen. If, on the other hand, Mary gave birth to a healthy child, Elizabeth's chances of becoming queen would recede sharply. When it became clear that Mary was not pregnant, no one believed any longer that she could have a child.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Elizabeth's succession seemed assured.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>King Philip, who ascended the Spanish throne in 1556, acknowledged the new political reality and cultivated his sister-in-law. She was a better ally than the chief alternative, <a href="/wiki/Mary,_Queen_of_Scots" title="Mary, Queen of Scots">Mary, Queen of Scots</a>, who had grown up in France and was betrothed to the <a href="/wiki/Francis_II_of_France" title="Francis II of France">Dauphin of France</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> When his wife fell ill in 1558, King Philip sent the <a href="/wiki/G%C3%B3mez_Su%C3%A1rez_de_Figueroa_y_C%C3%B3rdoba,_1st_Duke_of_Feria" title="Gómez Suárez de Figueroa y Córdoba, 1st Duke of Feria">Count of Feria</a> to consult with Elizabeth.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> This interview was conducted at Hatfield House, where she had returned to live in October 1555. By October 1558, Elizabeth was already making plans for her government. On 6 November, Mary recognised Elizabeth as her heir.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> On 17 November 1558, Mary died and Elizabeth succeeded to the throne. </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Accession">Accession</span></h2> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth_I_in_coronation_robes.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Elizabeth_I_in_coronation_robes.jpg/220px-Elizabeth_I_in_coronation_robes.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="295" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Elizabeth_I_in_coronation_robes.jpg/330px-Elizabeth_I_in_coronation_robes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Elizabeth_I_in_coronation_robes.jpg/440px-Elizabeth_I_in_coronation_robes.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2863" data-file-height="3845"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth_I_in_coronation_robes.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Elizabeth I in her coronation robes, patterned with 
         <a href="/wiki/Tudor_rose" title="Tudor rose">Tudor roses</a> and trimmed with 
         <a href="/wiki/Ermine" class="mw-redirect" title="Ermine">ermine</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Elizabeth became queen at the age of 25, and declared her intentions to her Council and other peers who had come to Hatfield to swear allegiance. The speech contains the first record of her adoption of the mediaeval <a href="/wiki/Political_theology" title="Political theology">political theology</a> of the sovereign's "two bodies": the body natural and the <a href="/wiki/Body_politic" title="Body politic">body politic</a>:<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> </p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>My lords, the law of nature moves me to sorrow for my sister; the burden that is fallen upon me makes me amazed, and yet, considering I am God's creature, ordained to obey His appointment, I will thereto yield, desiring from the bottom of my heart that I may have assistance of His grace to be the minister of His heavenly will in this office now committed to me. And as I am but one body naturally considered, though by His permission a body politic to govern, so shall I desire you all&nbsp;... to be assistant to me, that I with my ruling and you with your service may make a good account to Almighty God and leave some comfort to our posterity on earth. I mean to direct all my actions by good advice and counsel.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>As her <a href="/wiki/Triumphal_progress" class="mw-redirect" title="Triumphal progress">triumphal progress</a> wound through the city on the eve of the <a href="/wiki/Coronation_of_Elizabeth_I_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Coronation of Elizabeth I of England">coronation ceremony</a>, she was welcomed wholeheartedly by the citizens and greeted by orations and pageants, most with a strong Protestant flavour. Elizabeth's open and gracious responses endeared her to the spectators, who were "wonderfully ravished".<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> The following day, 15 January 1559, a date chosen by her astrologer <a href="/wiki/John_Dee" title="John Dee">John Dee</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-IHR_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IHR-43">[43]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Elizabeth was crowned and anointed by <a href="/wiki/Owen_Oglethorpe" title="Owen Oglethorpe">Owen Oglethorpe</a>, the Catholic <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Carlisle" title="Bishop of Carlisle">bishop of Carlisle</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a>. She was then presented for the people's acceptance, amidst a deafening noise of organs, fifes, trumpets, drums, and bells.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Although Elizabeth was welcomed as queen in England, the country was still in a state of anxiety over the perceived Catholic threat at home and overseas, as well as the choice of whom she would marry.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Church_settlement">Church settlement</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: 
       <a href="/wiki/Elizabethan_Religious_Settlement" title="Elizabethan Religious Settlement">Elizabethan Religious Settlement</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Nicholas_Hilliard_(called)_-_Portrait_of_Queen_Elizabeth_I_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Nicholas_Hilliard_%28called%29_-_Portrait_of_Queen_Elizabeth_I_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-Nicholas_Hilliard_%28called%29_-_Portrait_of_Queen_Elizabeth_I_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="286" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Nicholas_Hilliard_%28called%29_-_Portrait_of_Queen_Elizabeth_I_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Nicholas_Hilliard_%28called%29_-_Portrait_of_Queen_Elizabeth_I_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Nicholas_Hilliard_%28called%29_-_Portrait_of_Queen_Elizabeth_I_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-Nicholas_Hilliard_%28called%29_-_Portrait_of_Queen_Elizabeth_I_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3407" data-file-height="4427"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Nicholas_Hilliard_(called)_-_Portrait_of_Queen_Elizabeth_I_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>
         <i>The Pelican Portrait</i> by 
         <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Hilliard" title="Nicholas Hilliard">Nicholas Hilliard</a>. The 
         <a href="/wiki/Pelican" title="Pelican">pelican</a> was thought to nourish its young with its own blood and served to depict Elizabeth as the "mother of the Church of England".
         <sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Elizabeth's personal religious convictions have been much debated by scholars. She was a Protestant, but kept Catholic symbols (such as the crucifix), and downplayed the role of sermons in defiance of a key Protestant belief.<sup id="cite_ref-collinson_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-collinson-48">[48]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In terms of public policy she favoured pragmatism in dealing with religious matters. The question of her legitimacy was a key concern: although she was technically illegitimate under both Protestant and Catholic law, her retroactively-declared illegitimacy under the English church was not a serious bar compared to having never been legitimate as the Catholics claimed she was. For this reason alone, it was never in serious doubt that Elizabeth would embrace Protestantism. </p>
      <p>Elizabeth and her advisers perceived the threat of a Catholic crusade against heretical England. Elizabeth therefore sought a Protestant solution that would not offend Catholics too greatly while addressing the desires of English Protestants; she would not tolerate the more radical <a href="/wiki/Puritan" class="mw-redirect" title="Puritan">Puritans</a> though, who were pushing for far-reaching reforms.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> As a result, the parliament of 1559 started to legislate for a church based on the <a href="/wiki/Edward_VI_of_England#Reformation" title="Edward VI of England">Protestant settlement of Edward VI</a>, with the monarch as its head, but with many Catholic elements, such as vestments.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_England" title="House of Commons of England">House of Commons</a> backed the proposals strongly, but the bill of supremacy met opposition in the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a>, particularly from the bishops. Elizabeth was fortunate that many bishoprics were vacant at the time, including the <a href="/wiki/Archbishopric_of_Canterbury" class="mw-redirect" title="Archbishopric of Canterbury">Archbishopric of Canterbury</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> This enabled supporters amongst peers to outvote the bishops and conservative peers. Nevertheless, Elizabeth was forced to accept the title of <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Governor_of_the_Church_of_England" title="Supreme Governor of the Church of England">Supreme Governor of the Church of England</a> rather than the more contentious title of <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Head" class="mw-redirect" title="Supreme Head">Supreme Head</a>, which many thought unacceptable for a woman to bear. The new <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Supremacy_1559" class="mw-redirect" title="Act of Supremacy 1559">Act of Supremacy</a> became law on 8 May 1559. All public officials were to swear an oath of loyalty to the monarch as the supreme governor or risk disqualification from office; the <a href="/wiki/Heresy" title="Heresy">heresy</a> laws were repealed, to avoid a repeat of the persecution of dissenters practised by Mary. At the same time, a new <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Uniformity_1559" class="mw-redirect" title="Act of Uniformity 1559">Act of Uniformity</a> was passed, which made attendance at church and the use of an adapted version of the 1552 <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Common_Prayer" title="Book of Common Prayer">Book of Common Prayer</a> compulsory, though the penalties for <a href="/wiki/Recusancy" title="Recusancy">recusancy</a>, or failure to attend and conform, were not extreme.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Marriage_question">Marriage question</span></h2> 
      <p>From the start of Elizabeth's reign, it was expected that she would marry and the question arose to whom. Although she received many offers for her hand, she never married and was childless; the reasons for this are not clear. Historians have speculated that Thomas Seymour had put her off sexual relationships.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> She considered several suitors until she was about fifty. Her last courtship was with <a href="/wiki/Francis,_Duke_of_Anjou" title="Francis, Duke of Anjou">Francis, Duke of Anjou</a>, 22 years her junior. While risking possible loss of power like her sister, who played into the hands of King <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Spain" title="Philip II of Spain">Philip II of Spain</a>, marriage offered the chance of an heir.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> However, the choice of a husband might also provoke political instability or even insurrection.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Robert_Dudley">Robert Dudley</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth_and_Leicester_miniatures_by_Hilliard.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Elizabeth_and_Leicester_miniatures_by_Hilliard.png/220px-Elizabeth_and_Leicester_miniatures_by_Hilliard.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="125" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Elizabeth_and_Leicester_miniatures_by_Hilliard.png/330px-Elizabeth_and_Leicester_miniatures_by_Hilliard.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c6/Elizabeth_and_Leicester_miniatures_by_Hilliard.png 2x" data-file-width="440" data-file-height="250"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth_and_Leicester_miniatures_by_Hilliard.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Pair of 
         <a href="/wiki/Portrait_miniature" title="Portrait miniature">miniatures</a> of Elizabeth and Leicester, c.&nbsp;1575, by 
         <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Hilliard" title="Nicholas Hilliard">Nicholas Hilliard</a>. Their friendship lasted for over thirty years, until his death.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In the spring of 1559, it became evident that Elizabeth was in love with her childhood friend <a href="/wiki/Robert_Dudley,_1st_Earl_of_Leicester" title="Robert Dudley, 1st Earl of Leicester">Robert Dudley</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> It was said that <a href="/wiki/Amy_Robsart" title="Amy Robsart">Amy Robsart</a>, his wife, was suffering from a "malady in one of her breasts" and that the Queen would like to marry Dudley if his wife should die.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> By the autumn of 1559, several foreign suitors were vying for Elizabeth's hand; their impatient envoys engaged in ever more scandalous talk and reported that a marriage with her <a href="/wiki/Favourite" title="Favourite">favourite</a> was not welcome in England:<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> "There is not a man who does not cry out on him and her with indignation&nbsp;... she will marry none but the favoured Robert."<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Amy Dudley died in September 1560, from a fall from a flight of stairs and, despite the coroner's <a href="/wiki/Inquest" title="Inquest">inquest</a> finding of accident, many people suspected Dudley of having arranged her death so that he could marry the queen.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> Elizabeth seriously considered marrying Dudley for some time. However, William Cecil, <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Throckmorton" title="Nicholas Throckmorton">Nicholas Throckmorton</a>, and some conservative <a href="/wiki/Peerage_of_England" title="Peerage of England">peers</a> made their disapproval unmistakably clear.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> There were even rumours that the nobility would rise if the marriage took place.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Among other marriage candidates being considered for the queen, Robert Dudley continued to be regarded as a possible candidate for nearly another decade.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Elizabeth was extremely jealous of his affections, even when she no longer meant to marry him herself.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> In 1564, Elizabeth raised Dudley to the peerage as <a href="/wiki/Earl_of_Leicester" title="Earl of Leicester">Earl of Leicester</a>. He finally remarried in 1578, to which the queen reacted with repeated scenes of displeasure and lifelong hatred towards his wife, <a href="/wiki/Lettice_Knollys" title="Lettice Knollys">Lettice Knollys</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Still, Dudley always "remained at the centre of [Elizabeth's] emotional life", as historian <a href="/wiki/Susan_Doran" title="Susan Doran">Susan Doran</a> has described the situation.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> He died shortly after the defeat of the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Armada" title="Spanish Armada">Armada</a>. After Elizabeth's own death, a note from him was found among her most personal belongings, marked "his last letter" in her handwriting.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_candidates">Foreign candidates</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Erik_XIV,_1533-1577,_king_of_Sweden_(Steven_van_der_Meulen)_-_Nationalmuseum_-_38906.tif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Erik_XIV%2C_1533-1577%2C_king_of_Sweden_%28Steven_van_der_Meulen%29_-_Nationalmuseum_-_38906.tif/lossy-page1-220px-Erik_XIV%2C_1533-1577%2C_king_of_Sweden_%28Steven_van_der_Meulen%29_-_Nationalmuseum_-_38906.tif.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="394" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Erik_XIV%2C_1533-1577%2C_king_of_Sweden_%28Steven_van_der_Meulen%29_-_Nationalmuseum_-_38906.tif/lossy-page1-330px-Erik_XIV%2C_1533-1577%2C_king_of_Sweden_%28Steven_van_der_Meulen%29_-_Nationalmuseum_-_38906.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Erik_XIV%2C_1533-1577%2C_king_of_Sweden_%28Steven_van_der_Meulen%29_-_Nationalmuseum_-_38906.tif/lossy-page1-440px-Erik_XIV%2C_1533-1577%2C_king_of_Sweden_%28Steven_van_der_Meulen%29_-_Nationalmuseum_-_38906.tif.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2184" data-file-height="3915"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Erik_XIV,_1533-1577,_king_of_Sweden_(Steven_van_der_Meulen)_-_Nationalmuseum_-_38906.tif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Portrait of 
         <a href="/wiki/Eric_XIV_of_Sweden" title="Eric XIV of Sweden">Eric XIV</a> sent to Queen Elizabeth, to further the negotiations regarding the marriage. By 
         <a href="/wiki/Steven_van_der_Meulen" title="Steven van der Meulen">Steven van der Meulen</a> 1561.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Marriage negotiations constituted a key element in Elizabeth's foreign policy.<sup id="cite_ref-haigh2_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-haigh2-70">[70]</a></sup> She turned down Philip's own hand early in 1559 but for several years entertained the proposal of King <a href="/wiki/Eric_XIV_of_Sweden" title="Eric XIV of Sweden">Eric XIV of Sweden</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> For several years she also seriously negotiated to marry Philip's cousin <a href="/wiki/Charles_II,_Archduke_of_Austria" title="Charles II, Archduke of Austria">Archduke Charles of Austria</a>. By 1569, relations with the Habsburgs had deteriorated, and Elizabeth considered marriage to two French <a href="/wiki/House_of_Valois" title="House of Valois">Valois</a> princes in turn, first <a href="/wiki/Henry_III_of_France" title="Henry III of France">Henry, Duke of Anjou</a>, and later, from 1572 to 1581, his brother <a href="/wiki/Francis,_Duke_of_Anjou" title="Francis, Duke of Anjou">Francis, Duke of Anjou</a>, formerly Duke of Alençon.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> This last proposal was tied to a planned alliance against Spanish control of the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Netherlands" title="Southern Netherlands">Southern Netherlands</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Elizabeth seems to have taken the courtship seriously for a time, and wore a frog-shaped earring that Anjou had sent her.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Nicholas_Hilliard_002.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Nicholas_Hilliard_002.jpg/150px-Nicholas_Hilliard_002.jpg" decoding="async" width="150" height="209" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Nicholas_Hilliard_002.jpg/225px-Nicholas_Hilliard_002.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Nicholas_Hilliard_002.jpg/300px-Nicholas_Hilliard_002.jpg 2x" data-file-width="323" data-file-height="450"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Nicholas_Hilliard_002.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The Duke of Anjou, by 
         <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Hilliard" title="Nicholas Hilliard">Nicholas Hilliard</a>. Elizabeth called him her "frog", finding him "not so deformed" as she had been led to expect.
         <sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In 1563, Elizabeth told an imperial envoy: "If I follow the inclination of my nature, it is this: beggar-woman and single, far rather than queen and married".<sup id="cite_ref-haigh2_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-haigh2-70">[70]</a></sup> Later in the year, following Elizabeth's illness with <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Succession_to_Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Succession to Elizabeth I of England">succession question</a> became a heated issue in Parliament. They urged the queen to marry or nominate an heir, to prevent a civil war upon her death. She refused to do either. In April she <a href="/wiki/Legislative_session#Procedure_in_Commonwealth_realms" title="Legislative session">prorogued</a> the Parliament, which did not reconvene until she needed its support to raise taxes in 1566. Having promised to marry previously, she told an unruly House: </p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>I will never break the word of a prince spoken in public place, for my honour's sake. And therefore I say again, I will marry as soon as I can conveniently, if God take not him away with whom I mind to marry, or myself, or else some other great let happen.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>By 1570, senior figures in the government privately accepted that Elizabeth would never marry or name a successor. William Cecil was already seeking solutions to the succession problem.<sup id="cite_ref-haigh2_70-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-haigh2-70">[70]</a></sup> For her failure to marry, Elizabeth was often accused of irresponsibility.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Her silence, however, strengthened her own political security: she knew that if she named an heir, her throne would be vulnerable to a coup; she remembered that the way "a second person, as I have been" had been used as the focus of plots against her predecessor.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Elizabeth's unmarried status inspired a cult of virginity. In poetry and portraiture, she was depicted as a virgin or a goddess or both, not as a normal woman.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> At first, only Elizabeth made a virtue of her virginity: in 1559, she told the Commons, "And, in the end, this shall be for me sufficient, that a marble stone shall declare that a queen, having reigned such a time, lived and died a virgin".<sup id="cite_ref-Hh_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hh-80">[80]</a></sup> Later on, poets and writers took up the theme and turned it into an <a href="/wiki/Iconography" title="Iconography">iconography</a> that exalted Elizabeth. Public tributes to the Virgin by 1578 acted as a coded assertion of opposition to the queen's marriage negotiations with the Duke of Alençon.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Ultimately, Elizabeth would insist she was married to her kingdom and subjects, under divine protection. In 1599, she spoke of "all my husbands, my good people".<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span id="Mary.2C_Queen_of_Scots"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Mary,_Queen_of_Scots">Mary, Queen of Scots</span></h2> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:MaryQueenofScotsMourning.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/MaryQueenofScotsMourning.jpg/170px-MaryQueenofScotsMourning.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="225" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/MaryQueenofScotsMourning.jpg/255px-MaryQueenofScotsMourning.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/MaryQueenofScotsMourning.jpg/340px-MaryQueenofScotsMourning.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1511" data-file-height="2000"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:MaryQueenofScotsMourning.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Mary's French relatives considered her the rightful Queen of England and had the English arms emblazoned with those of Scotland and France.
         <sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Elizabeth's first policy toward <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Scotland" title="Kingdom of Scotland">Scotland</a> was to oppose the French presence there.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> She feared that the French planned to invade England and put her Catholic cousin <a href="/wiki/Mary,_Queen_of_Scots" title="Mary, Queen of Scots">Mary, Queen of Scots</a>, on the throne. Mary was considered by many to be the heir to the English crown, being the granddaughter of Henry VIII's elder sister, <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Tudor" title="Margaret Tudor">Margaret</a>. Mary boasted being "the nearest kinswoman she hath".<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Elizabeth was persuaded to send a force into Scotland to aid the Protestant rebels, and though the campaign was inept, the resulting <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Edinburgh" title="Treaty of Edinburgh">Treaty of Edinburgh</a> of July 1560 removed the French threat in the north.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> When Mary returned to Scotland in 1561 to take up the reins of power, the country had an established Protestant church and was run by a council of Protestant nobles supported by Elizabeth.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Mary refused to ratify the treaty.<sup id="cite_ref-loades_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-loades-89">[89]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1563 Elizabeth proposed her own suitor, Robert Dudley, as a husband for Mary, without asking either of the two people concerned. Both proved unenthusiastic,<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> and in 1565 Mary married <a href="/wiki/Henry_Stuart,_Lord_Darnley" title="Henry Stuart, Lord Darnley">Henry Stuart, Lord Darnley</a>, who carried his own claim to the English throne. The marriage was the first of a series of errors of judgement by Mary that handed the victory to the Scottish Protestants and to Elizabeth. Darnley quickly became unpopular and was murdered in February 1567 by conspirators almost certainly led by <a href="/wiki/James_Hepburn,_4th_Earl_of_Bothwell" title="James Hepburn, 4th Earl of Bothwell">James Hepburn, 4th Earl of Bothwell</a>. Shortly afterwards, on 15 May 1567, Mary married Bothwell, arousing suspicions that she had been party to the murder of her husband. Elizabeth confronted Mary about the marriage, writing to her: </p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>How could a worse choice be made for your honour than in such haste to marry such a subject, who besides other and notorious lacks, public fame has charged with the murder of your late husband, besides the touching of yourself also in some part, though we trust in that behalf falsely.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>These events led rapidly to Mary's defeat and imprisonment in <a href="/wiki/Loch_Leven_Castle" title="Loch Leven Castle">Loch Leven Castle</a>. The Scottish lords forced her to <a href="/wiki/Abdicate" class="mw-redirect" title="Abdicate">abdicate</a> in favour of her son <a href="/wiki/James_VI" class="mw-redirect" title="James VI">James VI</a>, who had been born in June 1566. James was taken to <a href="/wiki/Stirling_Castle" title="Stirling Castle">Stirling Castle</a> to be raised as a Protestant. Mary escaped from <a href="/wiki/Loch_Leven_(Kinross)" title="Loch Leven (Kinross)">Loch Leven</a> in 1568 but after another defeat fled across the border into England, where she had once been assured of support from Elizabeth. Elizabeth's first instinct was to restore her fellow monarch; but she and her council instead chose to play safe. Rather than risk returning Mary to Scotland with an English army or sending her to France and the Catholic enemies of England, they detained her in England, where she was imprisoned for the next nineteen years.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Catholic_cause">Catholic cause</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Sir_Francis_Walsingham_by_John_De_Critz_the_Elder.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Sir_Francis_Walsingham_by_John_De_Critz_the_Elder.jpg/170px-Sir_Francis_Walsingham_by_John_De_Critz_the_Elder.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="208" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Sir_Francis_Walsingham_by_John_De_Critz_the_Elder.jpg/255px-Sir_Francis_Walsingham_by_John_De_Critz_the_Elder.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Sir_Francis_Walsingham_by_John_De_Critz_the_Elder.jpg/340px-Sir_Francis_Walsingham_by_John_De_Critz_the_Elder.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="2940"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Sir_Francis_Walsingham_by_John_De_Critz_the_Elder.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Sir Francis Walsingham, Elizabeth's 
         <a href="/wiki/Spymaster" title="Spymaster">spymaster</a>, uncovered several plots against her life.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Mary was soon the focus for rebellion. In 1569 there was a major Catholic <a href="/wiki/Rising_of_the_North" title="Rising of the North">rising in the North</a>; the goal was to free Mary, marry her to <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Howard,_4th_Duke_of_Norfolk" title="Thomas Howard, 4th Duke of Norfolk">Thomas Howard, 4th Duke of Norfolk</a>, and put her on the English throne.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> After the rebels' defeat, over 750 of them were executed on Elizabeth's orders.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> In the belief that the revolt had been successful, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Pius_V" title="Pope Pius V">Pope Pius V</a> issued a <a href="/wiki/Papal_bull" title="Papal bull">bull</a> in 1570, titled <i><a href="/wiki/Regnans_in_Excelsis" title="Regnans in Excelsis">Regnans in Excelsis</a></i>, which declared "Elizabeth, the pretended Queen of England and the servant of crime" to be excommunicated and a <a href="/wiki/Heresy" title="Heresy">heretic</a>, releasing all her subjects from any allegiance to her.<sup id="cite_ref-McGrath,_69_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McGrath,_69-95">[95]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Collinson_p._67_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Collinson_p._67-96">[96]</a></sup> Catholics who obeyed her orders were threatened with <a href="/wiki/Excommunication" title="Excommunication">excommunication</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-McGrath,_69_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McGrath,_69-95">[95]</a></sup> The papal bull provoked legislative initiatives against Catholics by Parliament, which were, however, mitigated by Elizabeth's intervention.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> In 1581, to convert English subjects to Catholicism with "the intent" to withdraw them from their allegiance to Elizabeth was made a <a href="/wiki/High_treason" class="mw-redirect" title="High treason">treasonable offence</a>, carrying the death penalty.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> From the 1570s <a href="/wiki/Missionary" title="Missionary">missionary priests</a> from continental <a href="/wiki/Seminaries" class="mw-redirect" title="Seminaries">seminaries</a> went to England secretly in the cause of the "reconversion of England".<sup id="cite_ref-Collinson_p._67_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Collinson_p._67-96">[96]</a></sup> Many suffered execution, engendering a cult of <a href="/wiki/Martyrdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Martyrdom">martyrdom</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Collinson_p._67_96-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Collinson_p._67-96">[96]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><i>Regnans in Excelsis</i> gave English Catholics a strong incentive to look to Mary Stuart as the legitimate sovereign of England. Mary may not have been told of every Catholic plot to put her on the English throne, but from the <a href="/wiki/Ridolfi_Plot" class="mw-redirect" title="Ridolfi Plot">Ridolfi Plot</a> of 1571 (which caused Mary's suitor, the Duke of Norfolk, to lose his head) to the <a href="/wiki/Babington_Plot" title="Babington Plot">Babington Plot</a> of 1586, Elizabeth's spymaster Sir <a href="/wiki/Francis_Walsingham" title="Francis Walsingham">Francis Walsingham</a> and the royal council keenly assembled a case against her.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> At first, Elizabeth resisted calls for Mary's death. By late 1586, she had been persuaded to sanction her trial and execution on the evidence of letters written during the Babington Plot.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Elizabeth's proclamation of the sentence announced that "the said Mary, pretending title to the same Crown, had compassed and imagined within the same realm divers things tending to the hurt, death and destruction of our royal person."<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> On 8 February 1587, Mary was beheaded at <a href="/wiki/Fotheringhay_Castle" title="Fotheringhay Castle">Fotheringhay Castle</a>, Northamptonshire.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> After Mary's execution, Elizabeth claimed that she had not intended for the signed execution warrant to be dispatched, and blamed her Secretary, <a href="/wiki/William_Davison_(diplomat)" title="William Davison (diplomat)">William Davison</a>, for implementing it without her knowledge. The sincerity of Elizabeth's remorse and whether or not she wanted to delay the warrant have been called into question both by her contemporaries and later historians.<sup id="cite_ref-collinson_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-collinson-48">[48]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Wars_and_overseas_trade">Wars and overseas trade</span></h2> 
      <p>Elizabeth's foreign policy was largely defensive. The exception was the English occupation of <a href="/wiki/Le_Havre" title="Le Havre">Le Havre</a> from October 1562 to June 1563, which ended in failure when Elizabeth's <a href="/wiki/Huguenot" class="mw-redirect" title="Huguenot">Huguenot</a> allies joined with the Catholics to retake the port. Elizabeth's intention had been to exchange Le Havre for <a href="/wiki/Calais" title="Calais">Calais</a>, lost to France in January 1558.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> Only through the activities of her fleets did Elizabeth pursue an aggressive policy. This paid off in the war against Spain, 80% of which was fought at sea.<sup id="cite_ref-loades61_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-loades61-104">[104]</a></sup> She knighted <a href="/wiki/Francis_Drake" title="Francis Drake">Francis Drake</a> after his <a href="/wiki/Circumnavigation" title="Circumnavigation">circumnavigation</a> of the globe from 1577 to 1580, and he won fame for his raids on Spanish ports and fleets. An element of <a href="/wiki/Piracy" title="Piracy">piracy</a> and self-enrichment drove Elizabethan seafarers, over whom the queen had little control.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Netherlands">Netherlands</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Elzbieta_przyjmuj_ca_ambasador%C3%B3.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Elzbieta_przyjmuj_ca_ambasador%C3%B3.jpg/310px-Elzbieta_przyjmuj_ca_ambasador%C3%B3.jpg" decoding="async" width="310" height="203" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Elzbieta_przyjmuj_ca_ambasador%C3%B3.jpg/465px-Elzbieta_przyjmuj_ca_ambasador%C3%B3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Elzbieta_przyjmuj_ca_ambasador%C3%B3.jpg/620px-Elzbieta_przyjmuj_ca_ambasador%C3%B3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1071" data-file-height="700"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Elzbieta_przyjmuj_ca_ambasador%C3%B3.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Elizabeth receiving Dutch ambassadors, 1560s, attributed to 
         <a href="/wiki/Levina_Teerlinc" title="Levina Teerlinc">Levina Teerlinc</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>After the occupation and loss of <a href="/wiki/Le_Havre" title="Le Havre">Le Havre</a> in 1562–1563, Elizabeth avoided military expeditions on the continent until 1585, when she sent an English army to aid the Protestant <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Revolt" title="Dutch Revolt">Dutch rebels</a> against Philip II.<sup id="cite_ref-haigh135_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-haigh135-107">[107]</a></sup> This followed the deaths in 1584 of the allies <a href="/wiki/William_the_Silent" title="William the Silent">William the Silent</a>, Prince of Orange, and the Duke of Anjou, and the surrender of a series of Dutch towns to <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Farnese,_Duke_of_Parma" title="Alexander Farnese, Duke of Parma">Alexander Farnese, Duke of Parma</a>, Philip's governor of the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Netherlands" title="Southern Netherlands">Spanish Netherlands</a>. In December 1584, an alliance between Philip II and the French <a href="/wiki/Catholic_League_(French)" title="Catholic League (French)">Catholic League</a> at <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Joinville" title="Treaty of Joinville">Joinville</a> undermined the ability of Anjou's brother, <a href="/wiki/Henry_III_of_France" title="Henry III of France">Henry III of France</a>, to counter <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">Spanish</a> domination of the Netherlands. It also extended Spanish influence along the <a href="/wiki/English_Channel" title="English Channel">channel</a> coast of France, where the Catholic League was strong, and exposed England to invasion.<sup id="cite_ref-haigh135_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-haigh135-107">[107]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Antwerp_(1584%E2%80%931585)" class="mw-redirect" title="Siege of Antwerp (1584–1585)">siege of Antwerp</a> in the summer of 1585 by the Duke of Parma necessitated some reaction on the part of the English and the Dutch. The outcome was the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Nonsuch" title="Treaty of Nonsuch">Treaty of Nonsuch</a> of August 1585, in which Elizabeth promised military support to the Dutch.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> The treaty marked the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Anglo%E2%80%93Spanish_War_(1585)" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo–Spanish War (1585)">Anglo-Spanish War</a>, which lasted until the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_London_(1604)" title="Treaty of London (1604)">Treaty of London</a> in 1604. </p>
      <p>The expedition was led by her former suitor, the Earl of Leicester. Elizabeth from the start did not really back this course of action. Her strategy, to support the Dutch on the surface with an English army, while beginning secret peace talks with Spain within days of Leicester's arrival in Holland,<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> had necessarily to be at odds with Leicester's, who wanted and was expected by the Dutch to fight an active campaign. Elizabeth, on the other hand, wanted him "to avoid at all costs any decisive action with the enemy".<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> He enraged Elizabeth by accepting the post of Governor-General from the Dutch <a href="/wiki/States_General_of_the_Netherlands" title="States General of the Netherlands">States General</a>. Elizabeth saw this as a Dutch ploy to force her to accept sovereignty over the Netherlands,<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> which so far she had always declined. She wrote to Leicester: </p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>We could never have imagined (had we not seen it fall out in experience) that a man raised up by ourself and extraordinarily favoured by us, above any other subject of this land, would have in so contemptible a sort broken our commandment in a cause that so greatly touches us in honour&nbsp;... And therefore our express pleasure and commandment is that, all delays and excuses laid apart, you do presently upon the duty of your allegiance obey and fulfill whatsoever the bearer hereof shall direct you to do in our name. Whereof fail you not, as you will answer the contrary at your utmost peril.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>Elizabeth's "commandment" was that her emissary read out her letters of disapproval publicly before the Dutch Council of State, Leicester having to stand nearby.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> This public humiliation of her "Lieutenant-General" combined with her continued talks for a separate peace with Spain,<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> irreversibly undermined his standing among the Dutch. The military campaign was severely hampered by Elizabeth's repeated refusals to send promised funds for her starving soldiers. Her unwillingness to commit herself to the cause, Leicester's own shortcomings as a political and military leader, and the faction-ridden and chaotic situation of Dutch politics led to the failure of the campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> Leicester finally resigned his command in December 1587. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spanish_Armada">Spanish Armada</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:N%C3%ADcholas-Hilliard-Elizabeth-I-Queen-of-England-c-1586-87.tif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/N%C3%ADcholas-Hilliard-Elizabeth-I-Queen-of-England-c-1586-87.tif/lossy-page1-220px-N%C3%ADcholas-Hilliard-Elizabeth-I-Queen-of-England-c-1586-87.tif.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/N%C3%ADcholas-Hilliard-Elizabeth-I-Queen-of-England-c-1586-87.tif/lossy-page1-330px-N%C3%ADcholas-Hilliard-Elizabeth-I-Queen-of-England-c-1586-87.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/N%C3%ADcholas-Hilliard-Elizabeth-I-Queen-of-England-c-1586-87.tif/lossy-page1-440px-N%C3%ADcholas-Hilliard-Elizabeth-I-Queen-of-England-c-1586-87.tif.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2492" data-file-height="2495"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:N%C3%ADcholas-Hilliard-Elizabeth-I-Queen-of-England-c-1586-87.tif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Portrait from 1586–1587, by Nicholas Hilliard, around the time of the voyages of Sir 
         <a href="/wiki/Francis_Drake" title="Francis Drake">Francis Drake</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Meanwhile, Sir <a href="/wiki/Francis_Drake" title="Francis Drake">Francis Drake</a> had undertaken a major voyage against Spanish ports and ships in the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a> in 1585 and 1586. In 1587 he made a <a href="/wiki/Singeing_the_King_of_Spain%27s_Beard" title="Singeing the King of Spain's Beard">successful raid</a> on <a href="/wiki/Cadiz" class="mw-redirect" title="Cadiz">Cadiz</a>, destroying the Spanish fleet of war ships intended for the <i>Enterprise of England</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> as Philip II had decided to take the war to England.<sup id="cite_ref-haigh138_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-haigh138-117">[117]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>On 12 July 1588, the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Armada" title="Spanish Armada">Spanish Armada</a>, a great fleet of ships, set sail for the channel, planning to ferry a Spanish invasion force under the Duke of Parma to the coast of southeast England from the Netherlands. A combination of miscalculation,<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> misfortune, and an attack of English <a href="/wiki/Fire_ships" class="mw-redirect" title="Fire ships">fire ships</a> on 29 July off <a href="/wiki/Gravelines" title="Gravelines">Gravelines</a>, which dispersed the <a href="/wiki/Habsburg_Spain" title="Habsburg Spain">Spanish</a> ships to the northeast, defeated the Armada.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> The Armada straggled home to Spain in shattered remnants, after disastrous losses on the coast of Ireland (after some ships had tried to struggle back to Spain via the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a>, and then back south past the west coast of Ireland).<sup id="cite_ref-neale300_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-neale300-120">[120]</a></sup> Unaware of the Armada's fate, English militias mustered to defend the country under the Earl of Leicester's command. He invited Elizabeth to inspect her troops at <a href="/wiki/Tilbury" title="Tilbury">Tilbury</a> in Essex on 8 August. Wearing a silver breastplate over a white velvet dress, she addressed them in one of her <a href="/wiki/Speech_to_the_Troops_at_Tilbury" title="Speech to the Troops at Tilbury">most famous speeches</a>: </p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>My loving people, we have been persuaded by some that are careful of our safety, to take heed how we commit ourself to armed multitudes for fear of treachery; but I assure you, I do not desire to live to distrust my faithful and loving people&nbsp;... I know I have the body but of a weak and feeble woman, but I have the heart and stomach of a king, and of a King of England too, and think foul scorn that Parma or Spain, or any Prince of Europe should dare to invade the borders of my realm.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
      </blockquote> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth_I_(Armada_Portrait).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Elizabeth_I_%28Armada_Portrait%29.jpg/300px-Elizabeth_I_%28Armada_Portrait%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="240" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Elizabeth_I_%28Armada_Portrait%29.jpg/450px-Elizabeth_I_%28Armada_Portrait%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Elizabeth_I_%28Armada_Portrait%29.jpg/600px-Elizabeth_I_%28Armada_Portrait%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1248" data-file-height="1000"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth_I_(Armada_Portrait).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Portrait commemorating the defeat of the 
         <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Armada" title="Spanish Armada">Spanish Armada</a>, depicted in the background. Elizabeth's hand rests on the globe, symbolising her international power. One of three known versions of the "
         <a href="/wiki/Armada_Portrait" title="Armada Portrait">Armada Portrait</a>".
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>When no invasion came, the nation rejoiced. Elizabeth's procession to a thanksgiving service at <a href="/wiki/Old_St_Paul%27s_Cathedral" title="Old St Paul's Cathedral">St Paul's Cathedral</a> rivalled that of her coronation as a spectacle.<sup id="cite_ref-neale300_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-neale300-120">[120]</a></sup> The defeat of the armada was a potent propaganda victory, both for Elizabeth and for Protestant England. The English took their delivery as a symbol of God's favour and of the nation's inviolability under a virgin queen.<sup id="cite_ref-loades61_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-loades61-104">[104]</a></sup> However, the victory was not a turning point in the war, which continued and often favoured Spain.<sup id="cite_ref-xucglh_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-xucglh-122">[122]</a></sup> The Spanish still controlled the southern provinces of the Netherlands, and the threat of invasion remained.<sup id="cite_ref-haigh138_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-haigh138-117">[117]</a></sup> Sir <a href="/wiki/Walter_Raleigh" title="Walter Raleigh">Walter Raleigh</a> claimed after her death that Elizabeth's caution had impeded the war against Spain: </p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>If the late queen would have believed her men of war as she did her scribes, we had in her time beaten that great empire in pieces and made their kings of figs and oranges as in old times. But her Majesty did all by halves, and by petty invasions taught the Spaniard how to defend himself, and to see his own weakness.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup></p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>Though some historians have criticised Elizabeth on similar grounds,<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> Raleigh's verdict has more often been judged unfair. Elizabeth had good reason not to place too much trust in her commanders, who once in action tended, as she put it herself, "to be transported with an haviour of vainglory".<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1589, the year after the Spanish Armada, Elizabeth sent to Spain the <i><a href="/wiki/English_Armada" title="English Armada">English Armada</a></i> or <i>Counter Armada</i> with 23,375 men and 150 ships, led by Sir <a href="/wiki/Francis_Drake" title="Francis Drake">Francis Drake</a> as admiral and Sir <a href="/wiki/John_Norreys" class="mw-redirect" title="John Norreys">John Norreys</a> as general. The English fleet suffered a catastrophic defeat with 11,000–15,000 killed, wounded or died of disease<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fernández_Duro_1972_p.51_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fernández_Duro_1972_p.51-128">[128]</a></sup> and 40 ships sunk or captured.<sup id="cite_ref-Fernández_Duro_1972_p.51_128-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fernández_Duro_1972_p.51-128">[128]</a></sup> The advantage England had won upon the destruction of the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Armada" title="Spanish Armada">Spanish Armada</a> was lost, and the Spanish victory marked a revival of <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Spain" title="Philip II of Spain">Philip II</a>'s naval power through the next decade.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="France">France</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:England_Queen_Elizabeth_I_sixpence_1593.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/England_Queen_Elizabeth_I_sixpence_1593.jpg/220px-England_Queen_Elizabeth_I_sixpence_1593.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="110" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/England_Queen_Elizabeth_I_sixpence_1593.jpg/330px-England_Queen_Elizabeth_I_sixpence_1593.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/England_Queen_Elizabeth_I_sixpence_1593.jpg/440px-England_Queen_Elizabeth_I_sixpence_1593.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1100" data-file-height="550"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:England_Queen_Elizabeth_I_sixpence_1593.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Silver 
         <a href="/wiki/Sixpence_(British_coin)" title="Sixpence (British coin)">sixpence</a>, struck 1593, identifying Elizabeth as "
