         <a href="/wiki/By_the_Grace_of_God" title="By the Grace of God">by the Grace of God</a> Queen of England, 
         <a href="/wiki/English_claim_to_the_French_throne" class="mw-redirect" title="English claim to the French throne">France</a>, and Ireland"
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>When the Protestant <a href="/wiki/Henry_IV_of_France" title="Henry IV of France">Henry IV</a> inherited the French throne in 1589, Elizabeth sent him military support. It was her first venture into France since the retreat from Le Havre in 1563. Henry's succession was strongly contested by the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_League_(French)" title="Catholic League (French)">Catholic League</a> and by Philip II, and Elizabeth feared a Spanish takeover of the channel ports. The subsequent English campaigns in France, however, were disorganised and ineffective.<sup id="cite_ref-haigh142_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-haigh142-130">[130]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Peregrine_Bertie,_13th_Baron_Willoughby_de_Eresby" title="Peregrine Bertie, 13th Baron Willoughby de Eresby">Lord Willoughby</a>, largely ignoring Elizabeth's orders, roamed northern France to little effect, with an army of 4,000 men. He withdrew in disarray in December 1589, having lost half his troops. In 1591, the campaign of <a href="/wiki/John_Norreys" class="mw-redirect" title="John Norreys">John Norreys</a>, who led 3,000 men to <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">Brittany</a>, was even more of a disaster. As for all such expeditions, Elizabeth was unwilling to invest in the supplies and reinforcements requested by the commanders. Norreys left for London to plead in person for more support. In his absence, a Catholic League army almost destroyed the remains of his army at <a href="/wiki/Craon,_Mayenne" title="Craon, Mayenne">Craon</a>, north-west France, in May 1591. In July, Elizabeth sent out another force under <a href="/wiki/Robert_Devereux,_2nd_Earl_of_Essex" title="Robert Devereux, 2nd Earl of Essex">Robert Devereux, 2nd Earl of Essex</a>, to help Henry IV in besieging <a href="/wiki/Rouen" title="Rouen">Rouen</a>. The result was just as dismal. Essex accomplished nothing and returned home in January 1592. Henry abandoned the siege in April.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> As usual, Elizabeth lacked control over her commanders once they were abroad. "Where he is, or what he doth, or what he is to do," she wrote of Essex, "we are ignorant".<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ireland">Ireland</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: 
       <a href="/wiki/Tudor_conquest_of_Ireland" title="Tudor conquest of Ireland">Tudor conquest of Ireland</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:The_Image_of_Irelande_-_plate12.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/The_Image_of_Irelande_-_plate12.jpg/250px-The_Image_of_Irelande_-_plate12.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/The_Image_of_Irelande_-_plate12.jpg/375px-The_Image_of_Irelande_-_plate12.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/The_Image_of_Irelande_-_plate12.jpg/500px-The_Image_of_Irelande_-_plate12.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1417" data-file-height="908"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:The_Image_of_Irelande_-_plate12.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The 
         <a href="/wiki/Irish_people" title="Irish people">Irish</a> 
         <a href="/wiki/Gaels" title="Gaels">Gaelic</a> chieftain 
         <a href="/wiki/Turlough_Luineach_O%27Neill" title="Turlough Luineach O'Neill">O'Neale</a> and the other 
         <a href="/wiki/Kern_(soldier)" title="Kern (soldier)">kerns</a> kneel to 
         <a href="/wiki/Sir_Henry_Sidney" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Henry Sidney">Sir Henry Sidney</a> in submission.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Although Ireland was one of her two kingdoms, Elizabeth faced a hostile, and in places virtually autonomous,<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> Irish population that adhered to Catholicism and was willing to defy her authority and plot with her enemies. Her policy there was to grant land to her courtiers and prevent the rebels from giving Spain a base from which to attack England.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> In the course of a series of uprisings, Crown forces pursued <a href="/wiki/Scorched_earth" title="Scorched earth">scorched-earth</a> tactics, burning the land and slaughtering man, woman and child. During a revolt in <a href="/wiki/Munster" title="Munster">Munster</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Gerald_FitzGerald,_15th_Earl_of_Desmond" title="Gerald FitzGerald, 15th Earl of Desmond">Gerald FitzGerald, 15th Earl of Desmond</a>, in 1582, an estimated 30,000 Irish people starved to death. The poet and colonist <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Spenser" title="Edmund Spenser">Edmund Spenser</a> wrote that the victims "were brought to such wretchedness as that any stony heart would have rued the same".<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> Elizabeth advised her commanders that the Irish, "that rude and barbarous nation", be well treated; but she showed no remorse when force and bloodshed were deemed necessary.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Between 1594 and 1603, Elizabeth faced her most severe test in Ireland during the <a href="/wiki/Nine_Years%27_War_(Ireland)" title="Nine Years' War (Ireland)">Nine Years' War</a>, a revolt that took place at the height of hostilities with <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Spanish_War_(1585%E2%80%931604)" title="Anglo-Spanish War (1585–1604)">Spain</a>, who backed the rebel leader, <a href="/wiki/Hugh_O%27Neill,_Earl_of_Tyrone" title="Hugh O'Neill, Earl of Tyrone">Hugh O'Neill, Earl of Tyrone</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> In spring 1599, Elizabeth sent <a href="/wiki/Essex_in_Ireland" title="Essex in Ireland">Robert Devereux, 2nd Earl of Essex</a>, to put the revolt down. To her frustration,<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> he made little progress and returned to England in defiance of her orders. He was replaced by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Blount,_8th_Baron_Mountjoy" title="Charles Blount, 8th Baron Mountjoy">Charles Blount, Lord Mountjoy</a>, who took three years to defeat the rebels. O'Neill finally surrendered in 1603, a few days after Elizabeth's death.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> Soon afterwards, a peace treaty was signed between England and Spain. </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Russia">Russia</span></h3> 
      <p>Elizabeth continued to maintain the diplomatic relations with the <a href="/wiki/Tsardom_of_Russia" title="Tsardom of Russia">Tsardom of Russia</a> originally established by her half-brother. She often wrote to <a href="/wiki/Ivan_the_Terrible" title="Ivan the Terrible">Ivan the Terrible</a> on amicable terms, though the Tsar was often annoyed by her focus on commerce rather than on the possibility of a military alliance. The Tsar even proposed to her once, and during his later reign, asked for a guarantee to be granted asylum in England should his rule be jeopardised.<sup id="cite_ref-muscovy_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-muscovy-140">[140]</a></sup> English merchant and explorer <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Jenkinson" title="Anthony Jenkinson">Anthony Jenkinson</a>, who began his career as a representative of the <a href="/wiki/Muscovy_Company" title="Muscovy Company">Muscovy Company</a>, became the queen's <a href="/wiki/List_of_ambassadors_of_the_Kingdom_of_England_to_Russia" title="List of ambassadors of the Kingdom of England to Russia">special ambassador</a> to the court of Ivan the Terrible.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> Upon Ivan's death in 1584, he was succeeded by his less-ambitious son <a href="/wiki/Feodor_I_of_Russia" title="Feodor I of Russia">Feodor</a>. Unlike his father, Feodor had no enthusiasm in maintaining exclusive trading rights with England. Feodor declared his kingdom open to all foreigners, and dismissed the English ambassador Sir <a href="/wiki/Jerome_Bowes" title="Jerome Bowes">Jerome Bowes</a>, whose pomposity had been tolerated by Ivan. Elizabeth sent a new ambassador, Dr. Giles Fletcher, to demand from the regent <a href="/wiki/Boris_Godunov" title="Boris Godunov">Boris Godunov</a> that he convince the Tsar to reconsider. The negotiations failed, due to Fletcher addressing Feodor with two of his many titles omitted. Elizabeth continued to appeal to Feodor in half appealing, half reproachful letters. She proposed an alliance, something which she had refused to do when offered one by Feodor's father, but was turned down.<sup id="cite_ref-muscovy_140-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-muscovy-140">[140]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Muslim_states">Muslim states</span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:MoorishAmbassador_to_Elizabeth_I.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/MoorishAmbassador_to_Elizabeth_I.jpg/170px-MoorishAmbassador_to_Elizabeth_I.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="225" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/MoorishAmbassador_to_Elizabeth_I.jpg/255px-MoorishAmbassador_to_Elizabeth_I.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/MoorishAmbassador_to_Elizabeth_I.jpg/340px-MoorishAmbassador_to_Elizabeth_I.jpg 2x" data-file-width="772" data-file-height="1024"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:MoorishAmbassador_to_Elizabeth_I.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Abd el-Ouahed ben Messaoud was the Moorish ambassador to Elizabeth in 1600.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Trade and diplomatic relations developed between England and the <a href="/wiki/Barbary_states" class="mw-redirect" title="Barbary states">Barbary states</a> during the rule of Elizabeth.<sup id="cite_ref-vaughan_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vaughan-142">[142]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> England established a trading relationship with <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a> in opposition to Spain, selling armour, ammunition, timber, and metal in exchange for Moroccan sugar, in spite of a <a href="/wiki/Pope" title="Pope">Papal</a> ban.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> In 1600, <a href="/wiki/Abd_el-Ouahed_ben_Messaoud" title="Abd el-Ouahed ben Messaoud">Abd el-Ouahed ben Messaoud</a>, the principal secretary to the Moroccan ruler <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_al-Mansur" title="Ahmad al-Mansur">Mulai Ahmad al-Mansur</a>, visited England as an ambassador to the court of Queen Elizabeth I,<sup id="cite_ref-vaughan_142-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vaughan-142">[142]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> to negotiate an <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Moroccan_alliance" title="Anglo-Moroccan alliance">Anglo-Moroccan alliance</a> against Spain.<sup id="cite_ref-tate.org.uk_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tate.org.uk-146">[146]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-vaughan_142-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vaughan-142">[142]</a></sup> Elizabeth "agreed to sell munitions supplies to Morocco, and she and Mulai Ahmad al-Mansur talked on and off about mounting a joint operation against the Spanish".<sup id="cite_ref-Kupperman39_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kupperman39-147">[147]</a></sup> Discussions, however, remained inconclusive, and both rulers died within two years of the embassy.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Diplomatic relations were also established with the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> with the chartering of the <a href="/wiki/Levant_Company" title="Levant Company">Levant Company</a> and the dispatch of the first English ambassador to the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Porte" class="mw-redirect" title="Ottoman Porte">Porte</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Harborne" title="William Harborne">William Harborne</a>, in 1578.<sup id="cite_ref-Kupperman39_147-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kupperman39-147">[147]</a></sup> For the first time, a Treaty of Commerce was signed in 1580.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> Numerous envoys were dispatched in both directions and epistolar exchanges occurred between Elizabeth and Sultan <a href="/wiki/Murad_III" title="Murad III">Murad III</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Kupperman39_147-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kupperman39-147">[147]</a></sup> In one correspondence, Murad entertained the notion that <a href="/wiki/Islam_and_Protestantism" class="mw-redirect" title="Islam and Protestantism">Islam and Protestantism</a> had "much more in common than either did with Roman Catholicism, as both rejected the worship of idols", and argued for an alliance between England and the Ottoman Empire.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> To the dismay of Catholic Europe, England exported tin and lead (for cannon-casting) and ammunitions to the Ottoman Empire, and Elizabeth seriously discussed joint military operations with <a href="/wiki/Murad_III" title="Murad III">Murad III</a> during the outbreak of war with Spain in 1585, as Francis Walsingham was lobbying for a direct Ottoman military involvement against the common Spanish enemy.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_years">Later years</span></h2> 
      <p>The period after the defeat of the Spanish Armada in 1588 brought new difficulties for Elizabeth that lasted until the end of her reign.<sup id="cite_ref-xucglh_122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-xucglh-122">[122]</a></sup> The conflicts with Spain and in Ireland dragged on, the tax burden grew heavier, and the economy was hit by poor harvests and the cost of war. Prices rose and the standard of living fell.<sup id="cite_ref-haigh_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-haigh-152">[152]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-xucglh_122-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-xucglh-122">[122]</a></sup> During this time, repression of Catholics intensified, and Elizabeth authorised commissions in 1591 to interrogate and monitor Catholic householders.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> To maintain the illusion of peace and prosperity, she increasingly relied on internal spies and propaganda.<sup id="cite_ref-haigh_152-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-haigh-152">[152]</a></sup> In her last years, mounting criticism reflected a decline in the public's affection for her.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Devereaux_essex4.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Robert Devereaux, 2nd Earl of Essex" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Devereaux_essex4.jpg/170px-Devereaux_essex4.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="235" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Devereaux_essex4.jpg/255px-Devereaux_essex4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Devereaux_essex4.jpg/340px-Devereaux_essex4.jpg 2x" data-file-width="652" data-file-height="900"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Devereaux_essex4.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Lord Essex was a favourite of Elizabeth I despite his petulance and irresponsibility.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>One of the causes for this "second reign" of Elizabeth, as it is sometimes called,<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> was the changed character of Elizabeth's governing body, the <a href="/wiki/Privy_council" title="Privy council">privy council</a> in the 1590s. A new generation was in power. With the exception of Lord Burghley, the most important politicians had died around 1590: the Earl of Leicester in 1588; Sir Francis Walsingham in 1590; and Sir <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Hatton" title="Christopher Hatton">Christopher Hatton</a> in 1591.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> Factional strife in the government, which had not existed in a noteworthy form before the 1590s,<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> now became its hallmark.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> A&nbsp;bitter rivalry arose between the <a href="/wiki/Robert_Devereux,_2nd_Earl_of_Essex" title="Robert Devereux, 2nd Earl of Essex">Earl of Essex</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Cecil,_1st_Earl_of_Salisbury" title="Robert Cecil, 1st Earl of Salisbury">Robert Cecil</a>, son of Lord Burghley and their respective adherents, and the struggle for the most powerful positions in the state marred politics.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> The queen's personal authority was lessening,<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> as is shown in the 1594 affair of Dr. Lopez, her trusted physician. When he was wrongly accused by the Earl of Essex of treason out of personal pique, she could not prevent his execution, although she had been angry about his arrest and seems not to have believed in his guilt.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>During the last years of her reign, Elizabeth came to rely on the granting of monopolies as a cost-free system of patronage, rather than asking Parliament for more subsidies in a time of war.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> The practice soon led to <a href="/wiki/Price_fixing" title="Price fixing">price-fixing</a>, the enrichment of courtiers at the public's expense, and widespread resentment.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> This culminated in agitation in the House of Commons during the parliament of 1601.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> In her famous "<a href="/wiki/Golden_Speech" title="Golden Speech">Golden Speech</a>" of 30 November 1601 at <a href="/wiki/Whitehall_Palace" class="mw-redirect" title="Whitehall Palace">Whitehall Palace</a> to a deputation of 140 members, Elizabeth professed ignorance of the abuses, and won the members over with promises and her usual appeal to the emotions:<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> </p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>Who keeps their sovereign from the lapse of error, in which, by ignorance and not by intent they might have fallen, what thank they deserve, we know, though you may guess. And as nothing is more dear to us than the loving conservation of our subjects' hearts, what an undeserved doubt might we have incurred if the abusers of our liberality, the thrallers of our people, the wringers of the poor, had not been told us!<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup></p>
      </blockquote> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth_I_portrait,_Marcus_Gheeraerts_the_Younger_c.1595.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Elizabeth I in later years" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Elizabeth_I_portrait%2C_Marcus_Gheeraerts_the_Younger_c.1595.jpg/220px-Elizabeth_I_portrait%2C_Marcus_Gheeraerts_the_Younger_c.1595.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="296" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Elizabeth_I_portrait%2C_Marcus_Gheeraerts_the_Younger_c.1595.jpg/330px-Elizabeth_I_portrait%2C_Marcus_Gheeraerts_the_Younger_c.1595.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Elizabeth_I_portrait%2C_Marcus_Gheeraerts_the_Younger_c.1595.jpg/440px-Elizabeth_I_portrait%2C_Marcus_Gheeraerts_the_Younger_c.1595.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1296" data-file-height="1744"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth_I_portrait,_Marcus_Gheeraerts_the_Younger_c.1595.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Portrait of Elizabeth I attributed to 
         <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Gheeraerts_the_Younger" title="Marcus Gheeraerts the Younger">Marcus Gheeraerts the Younger</a> or his studio, c. 1595.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>This same period of economic and political uncertainty, however, produced an unsurpassed literary flowering in England.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> The first signs of a new literary movement had appeared at the end of the second decade of Elizabeth's reign, with <a href="/wiki/John_Lyly" title="John Lyly">John Lyly</a>'s <i>Euphues</i> and <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Spenser" title="Edmund Spenser">Edmund Spenser</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Shepheardes_Calender" title="The Shepheardes Calender">The Shepheardes Calender</a></i> in 1578. During the 1590s, some of the great names of <a href="/wiki/English_literature" title="English literature">English literature</a> entered their maturity, including <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Marlowe" title="Christopher Marlowe">Christopher Marlowe</a>. During this period and into the <a href="/wiki/Jacobean_era" title="Jacobean era">Jacobean era</a> that followed, the English theatre reached its highest peaks.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> The notion of a great <a href="/wiki/Elizabethan_era" title="Elizabethan era">Elizabethan era</a> depends largely on the builders, dramatists, poets, and musicians who were active during Elizabeth's reign. They owed little directly to the queen, who was never a major patron of the arts.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>As Elizabeth aged her image gradually changed. She was portrayed as <a href="/wiki/Belphoebe" title="Belphoebe">Belphoebe</a> or <a href="/wiki/Astraea_(mythology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Astraea (mythology)">Astraea</a>, and after the Armada, as <a href="/wiki/Gloriana" title="Gloriana">Gloriana</a>, the eternally youthful <a href="/wiki/Faerie_Queene" class="mw-redirect" title="Faerie Queene">Faerie Queene</a> of <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Spenser" title="Edmund Spenser">Edmund Spenser</a>'s poem. Elizabeth gave <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Spenser" title="Edmund Spenser">Edmund Spenser</a> a pension, as this was unusual for her, it indicates that she liked his work.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup> Her painted portraits became less realistic and more a set of enigmatic <a href="/wiki/Iconography" title="Iconography">icons</a> that made her look much younger than she was. In fact, her skin had been scarred by <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a> in 1562, leaving her half bald and dependent on wigs and cosmetics.<sup id="cite_ref-loades92_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-loades92-173">[173]</a></sup> Her love of sweets and fear of dentists contributed to severe tooth decay and loss to such an extent that foreign ambassadors had a hard time understanding her speech.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> André Hurault de Maisse, Ambassador Extraordinary from Henry IV of France, reported an audience with the queen, during which he noticed, "her teeth are very yellow and unequal&nbsp;... and on the left side less than on the right. Many of them are missing, so that one cannot understand her easily when she speaks quickly." Yet he added, "her figure is fair and tall and graceful in whatever she does; so far as may be she keeps her dignity, yet humbly and graciously withal."<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> Sir Walter Raleigh called her "a lady whom time had surprised".<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The more Elizabeth's beauty faded, the more her <a href="/wiki/Court_(royal)" class="mw-redirect" title="Court (royal)">courtiers</a> praised it.<sup id="cite_ref-loades92_173-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-loades92-173">[173]</a></sup> Elizabeth was happy to play the part,<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup> but it is possible that in the last decade of her life she began to believe her own performance. She became fond and indulgent of the charming but petulant young Robert Devereux, Earl of Essex, who was Leicester's stepson and took liberties with her for which she forgave him.<sup id="cite_ref-Lds_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lds-178">[178]</a></sup> She repeatedly appointed him to military posts despite his growing record of irresponsibility. After Essex's desertion of his command in Ireland in 1599, Elizabeth had him placed under house arrest and the following year deprived him of his monopolies.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> In February 1601, the earl tried to raise a rebellion in London. He intended to seize the queen but few rallied to his support, and he was beheaded on 25 February. Elizabeth knew that her own misjudgements were partly to blame for this turn of events. An observer wrote in 1602: "Her delight is to sit in the dark, and sometimes with shedding tears to bewail Essex."<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span></h2> 
      <p>Elizabeth's senior adviser, <a href="/wiki/William_Cecil,_1st_Baron_Burghley" title="William Cecil, 1st Baron Burghley">William Cecil, 1st Baron Burghley</a>, died on 4 August 1598. His political mantle passed to his son, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Cecil,_1st_Earl_of_Salisbury" title="Robert Cecil, 1st Earl of Salisbury">Robert Cecil</a>, who soon became the leader of the government.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup> One task he addressed was to prepare the way for a <a href="/wiki/Succession_to_Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Succession to Elizabeth I of England">smooth succession</a>. Since Elizabeth would never name her successor, Cecil was obliged to proceed in secret.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup> He therefore entered into a <a href="/wiki/Secret_correspondence_of_James_VI" title="Secret correspondence of James VI">coded negotiation</a> with <a href="/wiki/James_VI_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="James VI of Scotland">James VI of Scotland</a>, who had a strong but unrecognised claim.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup> Cecil coached the impatient James to humour Elizabeth and "secure the heart of the highest, to whose sex and quality nothing is so improper as either needless expostulations or over much curiosity in her own actions".<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup> The advice worked. James's tone delighted Elizabeth, who responded: "So trust I that you will not doubt but that your last letters are so acceptably taken as my thanks cannot be lacking for the same, but yield them to you in grateful sort".<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup> In historian J.&nbsp;E. Neale's view, Elizabeth may not have declared her wishes openly to James, but she made them known with "unmistakable if veiled phrases".<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:412px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Funeral_Elisabeth.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Funeral_Elisabeth.jpg/410px-Funeral_Elisabeth.jpg" decoding="async" width="410" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Funeral_Elisabeth.jpg/615px-Funeral_Elisabeth.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Funeral_Elisabeth.jpg/820px-Funeral_Elisabeth.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1182" data-file-height="454"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Funeral_Elisabeth.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Elizabeth's funeral cortège, 1603, with banners of her royal ancestors
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The Queen's health remained fair until the autumn of 1602, when a series of deaths among her friends plunged her into a severe depression. In February 1603, the death of <a href="/wiki/Catherine_Carey,_Countess_of_Nottingham" title="Catherine Carey, Countess of Nottingham">Catherine Carey, Countess of Nottingham</a>, the niece of her cousin and close friend <a href="/wiki/Catherine_Carey" title="Catherine Carey">Lady Knollys</a>, came as a particular blow. In March, Elizabeth fell sick and remained in a "settled and unremovable melancholy", and sat motionless on a cushion for hours on end.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup> When Robert Cecil told her that she must go to bed, she snapped: "Must is not a word to use to princes, little man." She died on 24 March 1603 at <a href="/wiki/Richmond_Palace" title="Richmond Palace">Richmond Palace</a>, between two and three in the morning. A few hours later, Cecil and the council set their plans in motion and proclaimed James King of England.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>While it has become normative to record the death of the Queen as occurring in 1603, following <a href="/wiki/Calendar_(New_Style)_Act_1750" title="Calendar (New Style) Act 1750">English calendar reform</a> in the 1750s, at the time England observed <a href="/wiki/New_Year%27s_Day" title="New Year's Day">New Year's Day</a> on 25 March, commonly known as <a href="/wiki/Lady_Day" title="Lady Day">Lady Day</a>. Thus Elizabeth died on the last day of the year 1602 in the old calendar. The modern convention is to use the old calendar for the date and month while using the new for the year.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth_I_of_England_grave_(left)_2013_crop2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Elizabeth_I_of_England_grave_%28left%29_2013_crop2.jpg/220px-Elizabeth_I_of_England_grave_%28left%29_2013_crop2.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Elizabeth_I_of_England_grave_%28left%29_2013_crop2.jpg/330px-Elizabeth_I_of_England_grave_%28left%29_2013_crop2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Elizabeth_I_of_England_grave_%28left%29_2013_crop2.jpg/440px-Elizabeth_I_of_England_grave_%28left%29_2013_crop2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="897" data-file-height="662"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth_I_of_England_grave_(left)_2013_crop2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Elizabeth as shown on her tomb at Westminster Abbey
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Elizabeth's coffin was carried downriver at night to <a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Whitehall" title="Palace of Whitehall">Whitehall</a>, on a barge lit with torches. At her funeral on 28 April, the coffin was taken to <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a> on a <a href="/wiki/Hearse" title="Hearse">hearse</a> drawn by four horses hung with black velvet. In the words of the chronicler <a href="/wiki/John_Stow" title="John Stow">John Stow</a>: </p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>Westminster was surcharged with multitudes of all sorts of people in their streets, houses, windows, leads and gutters, that came out to see the <a href="/wiki/Obsequy" class="mw-redirect" title="Obsequy">obsequy</a>, and when they beheld her statue lying upon the coffin, there was such a general sighing, groaning and weeping as the like hath not been seen or known in the memory of man.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup></p>
      </blockquote> 
      <p>Elizabeth was interred in Westminster Abbey, in a tomb shared with her half-sister, Mary I. The Latin inscription on their tomb, "Regno consortes &amp; urna, hic obdormimus Elizabetha et Maria sorores, in spe resurrectionis", translates to "Consorts in realm and tomb, here we sleep, Elizabeth and Mary, sisters, in hope of resurrection".<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: 
       <a href="/wiki/Cultural_depictions_of_Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Cultural depictions of Elizabeth I of England">Cultural depictions of Elizabeth I of England</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth-I-Allegorical-Po.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Elizabeth-I-Allegorical-Po.jpg/220px-Elizabeth-I-Allegorical-Po.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Elizabeth-I-Allegorical-Po.jpg/330px-Elizabeth-I-Allegorical-Po.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Elizabeth-I-Allegorical-Po.jpg/440px-Elizabeth-I-Allegorical-Po.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="2048"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Elizabeth-I-Allegorical-Po.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Elizabeth I, painted after 1620, during the first revival of interest in her reign. Time sleeps on her right and Death looks over her left shoulder; two 
         <a href="/wiki/Putto" title="Putto">putti</a> hold the crown above her head.
         <sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Elizabeth was lamented by many of her subjects, but others were relieved at her death.<sup id="cite_ref-Ld_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ld-193">[193]</a></sup> Expectations of King James started high but then declined, so by the 1620s there was a nostalgic revival of the cult of Elizabeth.<sup id="cite_ref-somerset726_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-somerset726-194">[194]</a></sup> Elizabeth was praised as a heroine of the Protestant cause and the ruler of a golden age. James was depicted as a Catholic sympathiser, presiding over a corrupt court.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup> The triumphalist image that Elizabeth had cultivated towards the end of her reign, against a background of factionalism and military and economic difficulties,<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup> was taken at face value and her reputation inflated. <a href="/wiki/Godfrey_Goodman" title="Godfrey Goodman">Godfrey Goodman</a>, Bishop of Gloucester, recalled: "When we had experience of a Scottish government, the Queen did seem to revive. Then was her memory much magnified."<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup> Elizabeth's reign became idealised as a time when crown, church and parliament had worked in constitutional balance.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The picture of Elizabeth painted by her Protestant admirers of the early 17th century has proved lasting and influential.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup> Her memory was also revived during the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a>, when the nation again found itself on the brink of invasion.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorian era</a>, the Elizabethan legend was adapted to the imperial ideology of the day,<sup id="cite_ref-Ld_193-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ld-193">[193]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup> and in the mid-20th century, Elizabeth was a romantic symbol of the national resistance to foreign threat.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup> Historians of that period, such as <a href="/wiki/J._E._Neale" title="J. E. Neale">J. E. Neale</a> (1934) and <a href="/wiki/A._L._Rowse" title="A. L. Rowse">A. L. Rowse</a> (1950), interpreted Elizabeth's reign as a golden age of progress.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup> Neale and Rowse also idealised the Queen personally: she always did everything right; her more unpleasant traits were ignored or explained as signs of stress.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Recent historians, however, have taken a more complicated view of Elizabeth.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup> Her reign is famous for the defeat of the Armada, and for successful raids against the Spanish, such as those on Cádiz in 1587 and 1596, but some historians point to military failures on land and at sea.<sup id="cite_ref-haigh142_130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-haigh142-130">[130]</a></sup> In Ireland, Elizabeth's forces ultimately prevailed, but their tactics stain her record.<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup> Rather than as a brave defender of the Protestant nations against Spain and the Habsburgs, she is more often regarded as cautious in her foreign policies. She offered very limited aid to foreign Protestants and failed to provide her commanders with the funds to make a difference abroad.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Elizabeth established an English church that helped shape a national identity and remains in place today.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup> Those who praised her later as a Protestant heroine overlooked her refusal to drop all practices of Catholic origin from the Church of England.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup> Historians note that in her day, strict Protestants regarded the <a href="/wiki/Elizabethan_Religious_Settlement" title="Elizabethan Religious Settlement">Acts of Settlement and Uniformity of 1559</a> as a compromise.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup> In fact, Elizabeth believed that faith was personal and did not wish, as <a href="/wiki/Francis_Bacon" title="Francis Bacon">Francis Bacon</a> put it, to "make windows into men's hearts and secret thoughts".<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Though Elizabeth followed a largely defensive foreign policy, her reign raised England's status abroad. "She is only a woman, only mistress of half an island," marvelled <a href="/wiki/Pope_Sixtus_V" title="Pope Sixtus V">Pope Sixtus V</a>, "and yet she makes herself feared by Spain, by France, by <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">the Empire</a>, by all".<sup id="cite_ref-somerset1_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-somerset1-217">[217]</a></sup> Under Elizabeth, the nation gained a new self-confidence and sense of sovereignty, as <a href="/wiki/Christendom" title="Christendom">Christendom</a> fragmented.<sup id="cite_ref-somerset726_194-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-somerset726-194">[194]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup> Elizabeth was the first Tudor to recognise that a monarch ruled by popular consent.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup> She therefore always worked with parliament and advisers she could trust to tell her the truth—a style of government that her Stuart successors failed to follow. Some historians have called her lucky;<sup id="cite_ref-somerset1_217-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-somerset1-217">[217]</a></sup> she believed that God was protecting her.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup> Priding herself on being "mere English",<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup> Elizabeth trusted in God, honest advice, and the love of her subjects for the success of her rule.<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup> In a prayer, she offered thanks to God that: </p> 
      <blockquote>
       <p>[At a time] when wars and seditions with grievous persecutions have vexed almost all kings and countries round about me, my reign hath been peacable, and my realm a receptacle to thy afflicted Church. The love of my people hath appeared firm, and the devices of my enemies frustrate.<sup id="cite_ref-somerset1_217-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-somerset1-217">[217]</a></sup></p>
      </blockquote>

        <li id="cite_note-156"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-156">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">John Cramsie, in reviewing the recent scholarship in 2003, argued "the period 1585–1603 is now recognised by scholars as distinctly more troubled than the first half of Elizabeth's long reign.         <li id="cite_note-198"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-198">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Dobson and Watson, 257.</span> </li> 
        <li id="cite_note-199"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-199">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Haigh, 175, 182.</span> </li> 
        <li id="cite_note-200"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-200">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Dobson and Watson, 258.</span> </li> 
        <li id="cite_note-201"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-201">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The age of Elizabeth was redrawn as one of <a href="/wiki/Chivalry" title="Chivalry">chivalry</a>, epitomised by courtly encounters between the queen and sea-dog "heroes" such as Drake and Raleigh. Some Victorian narratives, such as Raleigh laying his cloak before the queen or presenting her with a potato, remain part of the myth. Dobson and Watson, 258.</span> </li> 
        <li id="cite_note-202"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-202">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Haigh, 175.</span> </li> 
        <li id="cite_note-203"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-203">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In his preface to the 1952 reprint of <i>Queen Elizabeth I</i>, J.&nbsp;E. Neale observed: "The book was written before such words as "ideological", "fifth column", and "cold war" became current; and it is perhaps as well that they are not there. But the ideas are present, as is the idea of romantic leadership of a nation in peril, because they were present in Elizabethan times".</span> </li> 
        <li id="cite_note-204"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-204">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Haigh, 182.</span> </li> 
        <li id="cite_note-205"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-205">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Kenyon, 207</span> </li> 
        <li id="cite_note-206"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-206">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Haigh, 183.</span> </li> 
        <li id="cite_note-207"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-207">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Black, 408–409.</span> </li> 
        <li id="cite_note-208"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-208">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Haigh, 142–147, 174–177.</span> </li> 
        <li id="cite_note-209"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-209">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Loades, 46–50.</span> </li> 
        <li id="cite_note-210"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-210">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Weir, <i>Elizabeth</i>, 487.</span> </li> 
        <li id="cite_note-211"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-211">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Hogge, 9–10.</span> </li> 
        <li id="cite_note-212"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-212">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The new state religion was condemned at the time in such terms as "a cloaked papistry, or mingle mangle". Somerset, 102.</span> </li> 
        <li id="cite_note-213"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-213">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Haigh, 45–46, 177.</span> </li> 
        <li id="cite_note-214"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-214">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Black, 14–15.</span> </li> 
        <li id="cite_note-215"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-215">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Williams <i>Elizabeth</i>, 50.</span> </li> 
        <li id="cite_note-216"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-216">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Haigh, 42.</span> </li> 
        <li id="cite_note-somerset1-217"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-somerset1_217-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-somerset1_217-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-somerset1_217-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Somerset, 727.</span> </li> 
        <li id="cite_note-218"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-218">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Hogge, 9<i>n</i>.</span> </li> 
        <li id="cite_note-219"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-219">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Loades, 1.</span> </li> 
        <li id="cite_note-220"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-220">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">As Elizabeth's <a href="/wiki/Lord_Keeper_of_the_Great_Seal" title="Lord Keeper of the Great Seal">Lord Keeper</a>, Sir <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Bacon_(courtier)" class="mw-redirect" title="Nicholas Bacon (courtier)">Nicholas Bacon</a>, put it on her behalf to parliament in 1559, the queen "is not, nor ever meaneth to be, so wedded to her own will and fantasy that for the satisfaction thereof she will do anything&nbsp;... to bring any bondage or servitude to her people, or give any just occasion to them of any inward grudge whereby any tumults or stirs might arise as hath done of late days". Starkey <i>Elizabeth: Woman</i>, 7.</span> </li> 
        <li id="cite_note-221"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-221">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Somerset, 75–76.</span> </li> 
        <li id="cite_note-222"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-222">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Edwards, 205.</span> </li> 
        <li id="cite_note-223"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-223">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Starkey <i>Elizabeth: Woman</i>, 6–7.</span> </li> 
       </ol>

          <div>
           Elizabeth I of England 
          </div>
          <div>
           <b><a href="/wiki/House_of_Tudor" title="House of Tudor">House of Tudor</a></b>
          </div><span style="white-space:nowrap; font-size:90%; margin:2em"><b>Born:</b> 7 September 1533</span><span style="white-space:nowrap; font-size:90%; margin:2em">&nbsp;<b>Died:</b> 24 March 1603</span> </td>
        </tr>

         <td style="width: 40%; text-align: center;" rowspan="1"><b> <a href="/wiki/List_of_English_monarchs" title="List of English monarchs">Queen of England</a> and <a href="/wiki/King_of_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="King of Ireland">Ireland</a></b><br>1558–1603 </td>
